Of course, neither ending could explain all the plot elements so I guess it was left up to the audience to decide which parts of the film they wanted to pretend didn't happen. Personally, I would have left out the mumbo-jumbo about regression analysis - the alien thing was much more plausible than the Theodoric of York type of therapy. In any case the filmmakers shouldn't have wimped out, or at least made more of an attempt to make either ending compatible with what was presented.Despite another good performance by Spacey, I wouldn't recommend K-Pax - unless of course, you really like mentally writing your own scripts...</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Interesting premise spoiled by stupid direction</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Here's the pitch -- One Flew over the Cuckoo's Nest, with Kevin Spacey doing Nicholson as space alien, imparting new-age "wisdom" to benighted Earthlings.There's a contrived beginning, with Spacey's arrest for no reason, just so he can be admitted to a psycho ward, so Jeff Bridges can observe him. Bridges admits Spacey's no threat to anybody, which begs the question of why he's being detained. Ironically, Bridges, known for his mugging, is the only one in this thing who's NOT mugging. All the others, from Spacey to the bit players, throw stuff and work their faces and talk gibberish. Like Nicholson in Cuckoo's Nest, Spacey turns his ethnically diverse fellow inmates into hyper-conscious flower children: "Bluebird! Bluebird!" Suddenly, they're the congregation in a tent revival. He heals various loonies, mainly through his mere presence or some precious words of wisdom. At one point Spacey announces, "I can only take one person back with me." This is bad news for the audience. Bridges is assigned the role of Close Encounter's Richard Dreyfus who, as he begins to take Spacey seriously, is ostracized by his bratty kids and silly wife. But watch out, Spacey has a thing about water, which makes a scene in family Bridges's backyard. So Bridges's careerist bosses, despite testimonials form eminent scientists that he may truly be an alien, have an excuse to kick Spacey patient upstairs to the psycho ward, so Spacey disappears, until Bridges finds him perched in a tree, which gives Bridges a pretext for putting Spacey though a dreary couch scene so Spacey can showcase his talent for psychodrama.And on and on and on. A director with the finesse of a pair of boxing gloves has turned an intriguing "What if the crazy who thinks he's an alien really is an alien?" premise into a preachy, pedestrian soap opera. All the time and effort, all the wasted production values, all the pointless characters, the relentlessly ethereal soundtrack -- all, because some genius thought it would be clever to make a movie that plays on Kevin's last name. Pointless hubris, like Armand Hammer buying out the soda powder company.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Dumb Flew Over the Cuckoo's Nest</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Spoilers.Especially considering the caliber of its lead actors, Jeff Bridges and Kevin Spacey, K-PAX is an embarrassingly bad movie - high-concept, genre-mixing Hollywood at its cynical worst. Here's the pitch: "Hey, let's take the clichéd formula of the split personality psychological thriller and combine it with the benign alien SF visitation movie. We can have this alien, who comes from a civilization so advanced that he regards humans as a curious early-evolutionary species, make numerous light-beam visits to earth for the sole inexplicable purpose of befriending and rescuing a psychologically traumatized cattle-knocker from New Mexico (Spacey) by entering into his body and submitting to the indignities of a mental institution and repeated hypnosis. Meanwhile, we can also have this alien function as a super-philosopher/psychiatrist and - McMurphy-like - cure a host of quirky/lovable mental patients, not to mention his assigned doctor (Bridges) who of course has troubling neuroses of his own. We can also throw in a little Spielberg by having the alien run an essay contest to determine which one of the patients gets to win a free trip back to K-PAX." "Oh, that doesn't sound quite plausible? Well, let's not make it entirely clear that the alien IS an alien. Maybe the cattle-knocker is really a catatonic/idiot savant who somehow while living in a shack and working at the stocks managed to acquire astrophysical knowledge that befuddles the leading experts in the field and is barely revealed by the most advanced Hubble telescope. Whichever. It all ends happily with even the catatonic remnant being pushed in a wheelchair around the city and exhibiting a faint but clearly optimistic smile as his doctor pledges unending devotion to the cause of his cure." Really, this is stupid, stupid film making, insulting to both the actors and the audience. Even the satirically pitched scripts in Robert Altman's The Player (The Graduate II anyone?) sound intelligent by comparison.</fullReview>

		<username>tedg (tedg@filmsfolded.com)</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Mismanaged Ambiguity</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This came close to working for me. Partly that's because our designated internal watcher ? Jeff Bridges ? is so darn effective&gt; He's said he doesn't like these roles, that he would rather be in the story than watching it. But with "Lebowski" and "Fearless" he's become just about Hollywood's best.The setup is one that balances two genres, which means that it balances two universal urges, both with religious expressions avoided here.One is the great story of miraculous, supernatural grace.The other is the larger and more common (in film) of redemption. Both are ordinary, and the narrative novelty here is the ability to play them off against each other. The tension is that they are to some extent contradictory, and it is that tension that has sustained at least Catholic and Fundmentalis Christianity.On the one hand, the only path to redemption is hard work, pain, suffering. On the other, it is a rapturous instant, granted merely by believing ? in most versions it doesn't matter why you believe, only that you do.Is a tension that simmered during that dark ages and forms one of the great templates of Western narrative. Here recast in a direct framework. The possibilities are wonderful. The actor Spacey does understand this horizontal folding. (See him in "Hurlyburly.")And he does well enough to keep us from bolting. But the project is mismanaged. I think the reason why is simple: the studios want hope and in this case hope is on the side of random, ultimate salvation, miracles, lotterywinning. So the ambiguity is screwed, the tension dissipates and the wonder of the watcher is changed from something that matters, to something that simply assures.Ted's Evaluation -- 2 of 3: Has some interesting elements.</fullReview>

		<date>2002-05-09</date>
		<username>David Goody</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Enjoyable but not particularly involving</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Is there intelligent life within the mainstream film industry? We assume that there must be but sometimes it seems so hard to tell. K-PAX is the kind of film that reminds you what can be achieved with a little thought when there seems to be little else going on. Even though the closest we get to fireworks is the sight of some firecrackers on the fourth of July (that don't even go off) K-PAX is still strangely compelling.The film revolves around Prot, a man taken into psychiatric care when he appears out of nowhere at a New York railway station and claims he is a man from outer space. After a month of apathetically prescribed medication fails to show him the grim light of day (an ironic treatment since he was brought in as a drug-addicted delusional) he is handed over to psychiatrist Jeff Bridges who tries to convince him that he is as human as you or I, but as the sessions go on the good doctor starts to have some doubts.From the moment he appears on screen Kevin Spacey seems like something out of this world. Despite his appearance being merely a pair of sunglasses away from the dreary loser in the Shipping News, Spacey is full-on charisma mode and the camera can't help lapping it up. His body language, down to the look in often shielded eyes, and tone of his voice are note perfect and confirm the genius behind the man whose choices since American Beauty seem questionable at best.Whilst K-PAX bears superficial similarities to Brian Singer's seminal crime flick The Usual Suspects (we spend most of the film seeing Spacey spinning tales in an office) a far closer match is Don Juan DeMarco, where Johnny Depp's claim to be the world's greatest lover (which I'm sure many women easily accepted) stirs life into Marlon Brando's cynical psychoanalyst. As seems traditional Jeff Bridges brilliant doctor cannot sort his own family troubles (is this the Hollywood producers Faustian bargain for the piles of fees they poor in the LA analyst trade? That they will always be portrayed as hypocrites who ignore their wives). Mary McCormack brings great depth to the domestic scenes, with a performance whose knowingness spares us being bored with yet another cliched back story whilst perfectly illustrating every nuance of family life. Also refreshing is that Bridges remains restrained and we have no histrionics or snot-nosed tearful speeches of regret, only emotional ties straining at the dilemmas facing him.Where the film starts to falter is in the closing third when it starts paying serious attention to the whole is he/isn't he an alien finale. Whilst the ending itself is perfectly satisfactory, the build-up seems like cerebral chess played for pride rather a defining question of life and spirit. Perhaps this is where the performances are a little too calculated and methodical, all smooth smiles and no rough edges for the audience to latch on to. The viewer also starts to miss the laconic all-knowing cool Prot when we have him blubbing in hypnosis therapy, we lack enough role-models today, can't we have one who is happy deep down?The visual style of the film makes much play on the concept that Prot travelled on a beam of light to get to Earth and in the process sets a new record for the number of lens flares caught on camera (wasn't there a time when that was considered bad camerawork and not hideously cool? What next, having boom mikes in shot as a hip post-ironic film-literate touch?). However more than anything the films lingering visuals of daily life and nature around us give a feeling of being on the outside looking in at life, and reminding us of all the wonderful joys we miss when we lose our childlike wonder of the world.K-PAX stands as more than a demonstration of method acting mechanics but somehow less than a truly heartfelt film. Maybe the production got too caught up in Jeff Bridges easy going coolness, or maybe showing emotion is something they are not so good at doing on the planet K-PAX.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Critical Eye UK</username>
		<score>0.1</score>
		<shortDescription>Plan 10 From Outer Space. Rating: 1/10.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Warning: SPOILERSOK. Plot number one. A guy who works in a slaughter-house returns home to find that his wife and daughter have just been murdered by a drifter. The murderer's still on the premises so the distraught slaughter-house man strangles him. Then he walks out the door and jumps in a nearby river, never to be seen again.Oh wow; a fun narrative if ever there was -- but wait: just like buses, here comes plot number two. . .It's a couple of decades later, and NYPD take a crime witness into custody because he's wearing sunglasses and says he's from another planet. As around a quarter of everyone in New York wears sunglasses and is from another planet, it's not clear why this particular individual is singled out. Nor can the script trouble itself to explain why, when such people are usually dropped off in the most convenient doorway, the City instead decides to devote a half million dollarsworth of medical care to him.Ah, but! This is no ordinary alien. This is Kevin Spaceman. And the psychiatrist in charge of his case is no ordinary specialist, but Jeff ('Starman') Bridges. Not surprisingly then, a relationship quickly develops between the two, forged by dialog that seeks to surpass 'One Flew Over The Cuckoo's Nest' but can't quite make it to the standard of 'Home Alone 3'.Still. No matter that, as the script asserts, neither Buddha nor Christ knew what they were on about - huh? - this movie knows where it's headed, because not only does Spaceman eat an unskinned banana in close-up and Dolby Surround Sound, he goes on to describe the orbital dynamics of a planetary system so far removed from our galaxy that only the world's finest astronomers have any idea of its existence.And as he manages this feat by a complex series of mathematical formulae based on misrepresentations of the letters 'e' and 'm', well. That's it. Kevin Spaceman is either (a) definitely from the planet K-Pax or (b) a scientific genius the like of which has not been seen since Einstein. Hold the bananas. Give the guy the Nobel Prize instead.Starman Bridges, however, isn't convinced. . . and so journeys to New Mexico where by An Amazing Stroke of Fortune he gets taken by the local Sheriff to the abandoned and utterly unchanged home of a slaughterhouse worker whose wife and daughter were killed by a drifter, said drifter later being murdered by said slaughterhouse worker.By another Amazing Stroke of Fortune, Bridges suddenly develops a psychic ability greater than anything anyone else has ever possessed, including visitors from K-Pax, and so is able to 'see' Kevin Spaceman coming home after a hard day killing cows and finding that home is now just like his workplace. God. It's dreadful.Bridges rushes back to New York, where Kevin Spaceman's now doing a Jack Nicholson and showing his fellow mental patients that there's more to life than they thought, notably The Bluebird of Happiness, which is sitting on a tree outside their window.Anything, you feel, could happen now: Glenn Miller might land, or even Judy herself come back down from the far side of the rainbow.But instead, Kevin announces it's time for him to go back to planet K-Pax. Will he? Won't he?Naw. He merely becomes a catatonic wheelchair user, doomed to spend his days wearing impenetrable sunglasses and an enigmatic smile.Loyal to the end, Starman Bridges spends his time pushing Spaceman around New York, oblivious to the fortune that could be made from his case notes about a seriously traumatised New Mexican slaughterhouse worker who taught himself, er, astrophysics -- and went on to become The Cleverest Astronomer In The World.And that's it. No wonder so many reviewers here opted out of "analyzing" it. There's nothing to analyze.And no point in asking too many questions about it either, because Britain's FilmFour had a hand in it, and that's always as good a warning as you can get for the existence of pompous junk masquerading as entertainment.(Though of course, you do start to wonder at what stage in their respective careers Kevin Spaceman and Starman Bridges were lobotomised, because had they been of their usual sound mind even a cursory glance at the script would've confirmed how epically stupid `K-Pax' truly is.)And yet the movie does have some passing value, for to sit through it is to be reminded of just how brilliant, by comparison, was 'Plan 9 From Outer Space', where despite an absence of budget and even a leading man, Ed Wood managed to contrive something with greater logic, sharper narrative, and arguably better acting.VERDICT: 1/10 - though its pretensions are ludicrous and its contempt for an audience's intelligence risible, `K Pax' nevertheless earns a point for showing `Plan 9 From Outer Space' to be a genuine masterpiece.Come back Ed Wood. All is forgiven.</fullReview>

		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Well... Just can't miss a Kevin Spacey flick, but...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This is a weird flick. It's very intelligent in spots, and then just as you let your "intelligence assault" defenses down it clobbers you with either smarmy new-age religious views or thinly-veiled preaching about eating meat. I don't want to imply that this movie is terrible, it isn't, the performances are excellent and I did enjoy myself on the main. But if you're looking for another American Beauty, a smart, darkly funny flick that takes your intelligence seriously, you're probably going to be disappointed. I expected that because it was a Spacey flick and because it was a dialog flick. Maybe that was unrealistic of me.This movie takes place in a psych ward. Since I went to school for that (don't ask me why), I am a bit crippled by knowing too much about it, and just squirming at the way it's misrepresented for dramatic effect in this flick.Nobody, not even the stereotyped characters, turn in melodramatic performances, and I'm very glad for that. There's oone line that made me wince though, one where a tech was observing Spacey's vital signs and was talking to Bridges via an earpiece. "MY GOD MAN, BRING HIM OUT OF IT!" This is in reference to ordinary hypnosis, and in this movie "regression" is talked about in hushed tones like it's some big deal or some powerful tool, rather than something akin to phrenology. The line is delivered like Scotty talking from Engineering up to Kirk during a battle. I think that this particular example pretty well sums up what's wrong with the movie. If that would make you squirm, you're going to squirm quite a bit, just because the writer was either ignorant or counting on you being ignorant: it didn't have to be this way. Quite honestly the less you know the better off you are. That is the opposite of what an "intelligent movie" should be, and since this movie is almost pure dialog, it needs to be more intelligent than it is, frankly.The other thing is the script writer's thinly veiled ideological agenda. You're going to be clubbed over the head with anti-meat preaching. It's the typical delivery for zealots: they can't argue their position rationally, so they set up some more "highly evolved" personality that you're supposed to look up to to deliver the ideology from on high. Kinda like a Jack Chick tract only for the far-left instead of the far-right. It's smarmy, it's irritating, and frankly it's insulting. Capital punishment gets the same treatment. The writer doesn't like it, so you can be sure that the highly evolved denizens of the Universe know it to be the dumbest thing going. No discussion of why will be offered, of course. Spacey actually delivers a line about how nobody listens to Budda and Jesus, because they were against killing. The thing I find funniest about that is that I personally am agnostic, but I was raised in a Protestant church, and I seem to remember that Jesus guy saying something about selling your garments to buy a sword. And about dividing families two from three, and about not mistaking that he comes in peace, but that he comes bearing a sword. I'm digressing here but the basic fact is that either the writer of the book or the screenplay is just another post-modernist pseudointellectual who wants to take his personal ideology and elevate it artificially, and feed it to you. And if you're a thinking person, I don't think you're going to like that any more than I did.If you go in steeled for that, you'll probably like the flick okay. I gave it a six. If they'd left out the painfully transparent agenda I'd have given it an eight or even a nine.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Interesting at times but totally muddled at others ? falling into sentiment when all else fails (spoilers)</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>A man arrives at a train station but claims to have no ticket and have arrived from outer space. Naturally the police bundle him off to someone who cares, which in this case is the care of psychiatrist Mark Powell and his staff. At first it seems a clear cut case for Powell to solve with the only matter being how long it will take for the man calling himself Prot to come out of it. However some things cannot be easily explained and somehow parts of his story makes sense. With the deadline for Prot's return to the planet K-PAX approaching, Powell tries to get to the bottom of this man, unsure of what will happen when the deadline hits.The vaguely interesting premise drew me to this film and I did hope that it would be engaging as such. The opening sort of sums up the spirit of the film in the way it isn't clear whether Prot came in the light or just seen in the light. This was necessary for the mystery element of the story but sadly the film tries to have its cake and eat it ? keeping the mystery throughout while also winking at the audience to tell them whatever it is they want to hear. This does mean that the audience will all mostly get something they want but that nobody will get everything. So sci-fi interests will be served by Prot proving complex gravitational forces while others will be touched by the life messages delivered. This just about works until at some points it needs to clear up a bit and generally at these points the film tends to lapse into sentimentality and vagueness. It is annoying when it does so and it can be seen all through the film right up to Prot's slight smirk in the last shot.The cast do try reasonably hard despite this material. Spacey is a bit too wooden and he doesn't really help the film deliver this sense of mystery and mental complexity ? from his performance he seems sure that he is an alien, end of story. With this one-note performance he does what the film deserves but doesn't make it better (which he can do to a film). Bridges reverses his Starman role and does well enough. He delivers mixed feelings and is more interesting as a result. The starry support is impressive but generally they don't have a lot to do even if the presence of McCormack, Woodard and others is good. Fans of the Wire will welcome Gerety but lament Peters not being given more as he is a very good actor.Overall then a so-so film. It has enough to be of interest to a good range of viewers but it doesn't do that well with it and floats into vagueness or sentimentality regularly. Any actual movement in one direction is delivered with a wink to the audience to keep the mystery but this is tiresome after a while. Worth a look I suppose but not as good as the heavyweight cast would suggest.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>The opening is so creepy, I was soured on the rest of the movie. Since the harmless little goofball doesn't want (or seem to need) help, then why would he keep seeing the shrink. Well, we'll just lock him up . ..In the opening scene, Spacey (playing Prot, as we discover his name later) just appears in the middle of a New York City commuter rail terminal. He is a bystander when a little old lady gets her purse snatched. When the police question him, he calmly explains that he is from another planet. So far so good. But then THE POLICE ARREST HIM, CONFINE HIM FOR THREE WEEKS, AND SHOOT HIM FULL OF THORAZINE. Holy Cow! This kind of treatment of the harmlessly goofy has not even been seen in the wildest fantasies of Mayor Giuliani. Used as a throw away plot device, this kind of action is just creepy.We then meet Bridges (Dr. Powell) the shrink. Jeff must have had something in his contract that said something like he refused to portray an overworked, underpaid social worker. So instead, we see an obsessive, wealthy shrink that the City of New York has somehow forced to see this loveable goofball that they locked up in the scene before. So we get to see Bridges in a crisp shirt and tie in a spacious high-rise office that has, for some reason, something that looks like a pharmaceutical lab in the middle of it. I guess this is supposed to look "serious" and "scientific", but I fully expected one of the little geeks in white lab coats to come running around the corner with a smoking beaker full of blue stuff that the shrink could use to cure every psychotic in the world.The middle of the movie is pleasant enough, but there is not much to it. So we get to see a very long sequence where Spacey is riding in a cab. Some wonderful electronic trance music is scored and we get to see some beautiful shots of lemons falling out of boxes, but it was all just beautiful filler.Finally, I won't give the ending, but suffice it to say, the writer either has to decide that Prot is an alien or he is a human. The writer did not have the guts to follow through with the choice made throughout the last half of the movie and waffles at the end. Sigh. A fitting ending to not much of a movie.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>- This film really made me laughed. Because you can't take it to seriously. From the 1st scene in the NY station, the story looks so little plausible. An investigation about the identity of a guy called Prot will lead us to a supposedly thought-provoking quest. Please understand me, this film might appeal to you, it really did not get to me. If you have not seen the film, I suggest you do not read my comment, and if you have, then share my very subjective opinion.- Dr. Powell, a psychiatrist, tries to cure an enigmatic mental patient called Prot claiming to be an alien who will soon be heading to his planet K-PAX. Is he what he claims to be (possible considering his unexplained super-intelligent abilities) or is he just a damaged person living out a dissociative delusion (possible too because of psychological reasons).Despite troubling elements, the psychiatrist favors the second solution especially after he uncovers a man of similar description named Robert Porter who suffered a trauma five years earlier, the same day that Prot is claiming to be arrived on earth. But the patients of the hospital love Prot and seem to recover thanks to him. The investigation about the real identity of his patient will also help the workaholic psychiatrist redress his own failures in his family.- How will you reconcile these 2 contradictory views : Alien or suffering from delusion ? Guess the answer : we are left asking after a resourceful ending.- As Dr. Powell struggles with his own sense of reality, we are also asked to question ourself how this unconvincing drama will lift up our spiritual and reconsider our social beliefs. I am still asking too. The biggest outrage of this pretentious film is to make you think that you must have missed something big because you find the story so murky. - The film failed to move me because I cannot believe any situations, some moments were so predictable (don't tell me you did not guess that Mark Powell would not meet his son at the end) and performances are just clichés (especially people at the hospital and the wonky hypnosis sessions), the acting is both contrived and "already seen" and the luminescent cinematography doesn't make up for the clumsy script. My biggest disappointment however lies in the fact that the film wants to have its finger in more than one pie : an underlying criticism of curing mental illness and an apology for better social human contacts but it fails to give any valid answers. The film is even very prejudiced against mental ill people sometimes insinuating that mental illness is just a result of social ills. The film aims at being manipulative like "A beautiful mind" but it seems as if I could never bite (why should I care for the guy in New Mexico ?). The end becomes submerged in suggested moralistic overtones. The supposedly ambiguous ending come after so many evidences for both interpretations that you are bound to believe that Hollywood wanted to make a cross of Donnie darko + Pay it forward +"One Flew Over the Cuckoo's Nest" + E.T. + a few other ones- I love films that tackle sensitive issues that challenge our perceptions of reality : If you want to see more convincing dramas of this type : Please see "Donnie Darko" or even " With a Friend Like Harry..." . Far far better to my mind..Oh yes I have forgotten something very important : Besides, the film is a real bore to watch. - TO SUM UP : A PARADE OF BADLY USED MANIPULATIVE GIMMICKS AIMING AT TURNING THE VIEWER INTO AN INSANE ALIEN (3 out of 10)</fullReview>

		<date>2002-04-20</date>
		<username>dngrrkitn</username>
		<score>0.1</score>
		<shortDescription>An insulting waste of time</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>While most movies in their attempt to depict psychiatric treatment are mostly inaccurate, K-Pax breaks every rule from its very first scene. In this fantasy New York, people need not demonstrate dangerous behavior to be committed to long term inpatient psychiatric care, and while in this care they have ample access to letter openers, shoelaces, glassware, and scores of other self-harm tools. These units also believe in hypnosis as a legitimate therapeutic intervention, and clinicians discuss their personal lives with patients and even introduce them to family members.Unfortunately, the center of the movie is the so-called mystery of Prot's identity. At times each answer seems so obvious, but then the movie completely changes its mind and the audience is asked to believe the completely opposite explanation. Rather than being ambiguous, the plot ineptly lurches from idea to idea, with little rationale to link them. A few people might find this intriguing, but mostly it becomes more and more tiresome.The movie was made with a loving eye, and the cinematography is lovely and expertly done. Unfortunately, the same cannot be said for the acting. Jeff Bridges has two expressions: "befuddlement" and "intense concentration." Kevin Spacey acquits himself without too much embarrassment. The rest of the characters are ciphers, with one or two distinguishing traits which must substitute for personalities.I suppose this movie has a "message" -- something about living every day to the fullest, and loving one's family -- but, honestly, it has been done better in a Lifesavers ad. Watch that, and spend your free two hours with your family doing anything but watching this movie.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>kround</username>
		<score>0.1</score>
		<shortDescription>MORALLY IRRESPONSIBLE!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>While the acting is great and the premise is fascinating, the film-makers are morally irresponsible by contending that the right cure for mental illness is simply a matter of picking yourself up by your own bootstraps (e.g., Prot's explanation to the doctor regarding mental illness and the nature of the three tasks). I am not aware that anyone has recovered from OC by looking for a bluebird! This film is highly prejudiced against people who suffer from mental illness by attempting to affirm that old, worn-out joke "it's all in your head" and thereby asserting that mental illness is simply a personality flaw. The film-makers need to abandon their Victorian mindset or, even better, do the mentally ill a big favor and stop writing about things they don't understand and obviously didn't bother to research. The cinematic value of this film (which I believe is very high) is offset by its barbaric misconceptions. It's also highly derivative of One Flew Over the Cuckoo's Nest (1975), perpetuating its 26 year-old stereotypes.</fullReview>

The supporting cast doesn't belong in this movie, but they could make their own and it would be one thousand times better than this. Quirky supporting characters are a dime a dozen, but these were more interesting than the leads. Oh, wait. We saw them before in other movies. Never mind.K-PAX is designed to get those who fester into the theater and feel some kind of wonder. The wonder felt here is when is it going to be over? </fullReview>
